کا معنی یہ ہو گا قرآن پاک وہ ہے جو منقول بین دفتی المصحف ہو خواہ منقول مجموع من حیث المجموع کے اعتبار سے ہویا ہر ہر جزء کے اعتبار سے ہو اب دو صورتیں ہیں (1) النقل الینا میں جو ما ہے اس کو عموم پر رکھا جائے تو معنی یہ ہوگا کہ قران ہر وہ چیز ہے جو منقول بین وفق المصحف ہو خواہ مجموع من حیث المجموع ہو خواہ وہ کلمہ نام ہو خواہ وہ کلام ناقص ہو خواہ وہ ایک کلام ہو خواہ ایک حرف ہو تو اس صورت میں یہ تعریف حرف اور کلمہ پر بھی صادق آئے گی حالانکہ عرف شرعیت میں حرف اور کلمہ کو قرآن نہیں کہا جاتا تو تعریف مانع نہیں رہے گی (۲) اور اگر ما کو خاص کیا جائے اور اس سے مراد کلام تام لی جائے تو قرآن پاک خاص ہو جائے گا کلام نام کے ساتھ تو اس صورت میں بعض وہ کلام جو نام نہیں ہے قرآن پاک سے خارج ہو جائیں گی حالانکہ اسے قرآن کہا جاتا ہے اور اس پر احکام قرآن جاری ہوتے ہیں مثلا محدث کیلئے میں جائز نہیں جنسی کیلئے تلاوت جائز نہیں لہذا تعریف جامع نہیں رہے گی حاصل کلام ایں کہ اگر ما سے عام مراد ہو جو کل اور بعض دونوں کو شامل ہو تعریف مانع نہیں ہوگی اور اگر ما سے خاص کلام تام مراد ہو تو تعریف جامع نہیں ہوگی یہ تفصیل اس صورت میں ہے اگر یہ تعریف معنی کلی کیلئے ہو اور اگر غرض اصولی کو نہ دیکھا جائے بلکہ مصنف کی کلام کے سیاق کو دیکھا جائے تو پھر یہ تعریف مجموع شخصی کی ہوگی کیونکہ مصنف نے اس تعریف کو مجموع شخصی کی تعریف قرار دیا ہے تو اس صورت میں گذشتہ اعتراضات وارد نہیں ہونگے البتہ یہ اشکال وارد ہوگا کہ یہ غرض اصولی کے مناسب نہیں ہے کیونکہ غرض اصولی ہر ہر آیت اور جزء سے متعلق ہے نہ کہ مجموع من حیث الجوع ہے۔ جواب ! ہم شق ثانی اختیار کرتے ہیں کہ مصنف کی مراد مانقل سے مجموع من حیث المجموع الشخصی ہے باقی رہا آپ کا ئیہ اشکال کہ یہ غرض اصولی کے خلاف ہے تو ہم جواب عرض کرتے ہیں کہ عرض اصولی کی خلاف نہیں ہے کیونکہ اصولی کی غرض بھی مجموع من حیث المجموع سے متعلق ہے اس لیے کہ اصولی جو آیت سے استدلال کرتا ہے وہ بھی اس حیثیت سے کرتا ہے کہ وہ آیت اس مجموع کا ایک حصہ اور جزء ہے اس حیثیت سے استدلال نہیں کرتا کہ وہ عین کتاب اللہ ہے فلا اشکال عليه
Its meaning will be: The Holy Quran is what has been transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf, whether transmitted as a whole collectively or part by part. Now there are two possibilities: (1) If the word 'ma' in 'al-naql ilayna' is taken as general, then the meaning becomes: The Quran is everything that has been transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf, whether it is the whole collectively, or a complete expression, or an incomplete speech, or a single word, or even a single letter. In that case, this definition would apply to a letter and a word, whereas in the Shar'i usage a letter or a word is not called Quran, so the definition would not be exclusive (mani'). (2) If 'ma' is specified to mean complete speech, then the Quran would be restricted to complete expressions. In that case, some speech that is not a complete expression would be excluded from the Quran, yet it is called Quran and Quranic rulings apply to it—for example, it is not permissible for a menstruating woman to recite it, and not permissible for a junub to recite it. Therefore, the definition would not be comprehensive (jami'). The conclusion is: If by 'ma' a general meaning is intended that includes both the whole and the part, then the definition is not exclusive. If by 'ma' a specific complete speech is intended, then the definition is not comprehensive. This detail applies if this definition is for the general meaning. But if one does not consider the usuli purpose, rather looks at the context of the author's words, then this definition is for the particular whole (majmu' shakhsi), because the author made this definition for the particular whole. In that case, the previous objections do not arise. However, one objection remains: that this is not appropriate for the usuli purpose, because the usuli purpose concerns every verse and part, not the whole as a whole. Answer: We adopt the second option, that the author intends by 'ma nuqila' the particular whole as a whole. As for your objection that it contradicts the usuli purpose, we respond: It does not contradict the usuli purpose because the usuli purpose also relates to the whole as a whole. This is because when a usuli argues using a verse, he does so from the perspective that that verse is a part and portion of that whole, not from the perspective that it is exactly the Book of Allah. Hence, there is no problem.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the definition of the Quran: 'ما نقل إلينا بين دفتي المصحف' (what has been transmitted to us between the covers of the Mushaf). The commentator examines two interpretations of the word 'ما' (ma). If 'ما' is taken as a general relative pronoun, the definition would include parts like single letters, which is not correct because in shariah a single letter is not called Quran. Thus the definition fails to be exclusive (mane'). If 'ما' is taken as 'that which' referring only to complete utterances, then incomplete utterances (like some short verses) would be excluded, yet they are Quran and have rulings (e.g., not permissible for a menstruating woman to recite). So the definition fails to be comprehensive (jami'). The author then clarifies that if the definition is for the 'particular whole' (majmu' shakhsi), these objections disappear because the definition is not intended to cover parts. He further argues that even for usul purposes, the definition works because the usul considers verses as parts of the whole, not as independent. This is a typical Hanafi usul approach to definitions, emphasizing the need for precision to avoid both over-inclusion and under-inclusion.

Key Terms
ما نقل إلينا بين دفتي المصحف (ma nuqila ilayna bayna dafatay al-mushaf): The definition of the Quran under analysis: what has been transmitted to us between the covers of the Mushaf
مانع (mani'): Exclusive: a definition that excludes what should not be included
جامع (jami'): Comprehensive: a definition that includes all intended instances

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi analysis, what is the final resolution that makes the definition 'ما نقل إلينا بين دفتي المصحف' valid?
A) The word 'ما' is taken as a general relative pronoun.
B) The definition is applied to the particular whole (majmu' shakhsi) rather than the general meaning.
C) The definition is restricted to complete utterances only.
D) The definition is abandoned in favor of another.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi analysis shows that a definition's validity depends on its intended scope; the definition of the Quran works for the whole, not the parts, and this is compatible with the usuli purpose.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Why the Definition of the Qur'an is Tashkhis (Identification)
Explaining that the definition is tashkhis due to the dual meaning of Qur'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi usuli definition of the Qur'an is an identification (tashkhis) rather than a definition of quiddity (mahiyyah), and articulate the two meanings of the term 'Qur'an' (eternal speech vs. verbal speech).

Core Idea
The definition of the Qur'an is identification because the term applies to both eternal speech and recited words; usulis focus on the latter for rulings.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, when we define the Qur'an as 'the book transmitted between the two covers,' we are not defining its eternal essence but identifying the specific entity from which rulings are derived. This passage clarifies that due to the dual meaning of the word 'Qur'an' (the eternal attribute and the recited words), the definition serves to pinpoint the recited Qur'an, which is the subject of legal inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two meanings of 'Qur'an'
Identify the two senses: eternal speech (kalam azali) – an uncreated attribute of Allah; and verbal speech (kalam lafzi) – the created, recited words composed of letters and sounds. Note their different attributes.
The word 'Qur'an' is used for both, causing ambiguity that the definition must resolve.
2
Understand the usuli's concern
Recognize that usul al-fiqh deals with the verbal speech because legal rulings (ahkam) are derived from the recited text, not from the eternal attribute. The usuli considers the Qur'an as the establisher of rulings.
This explains why the definition targets the verbal speech.
3
Analyze why the definition is tashkhis
Observe that the definition mentions characteristics (e.g., 'between the two covers') to single out the recited Qur'an, distinguishing it from the eternal speech and from other texts. It is not meant to capture the quiddity of the Quran in an absolute sense.
Tashkhis means identification by specifying features.
4
Resolve the objection about equivalence
Note that a definition must be coextensive (musawi) with the defined. Mentioning only 'that which is transmitted' (manqul) distinguishes from eternal speech but does not cover all instances of the recited Qur'an (e.g., oral recitation without a mushaf). Additional restrictions like 'between the two covers' are needed to achieve full equivalence.
Thus the definition is comprehensive and precise.

اصل کلام اللہ اور قرآن تو وہی ہے جو صفہ قدیمہ ہے اور یہ کلام لفظی اس پر دال ہے اس سے عبارت ہے۔ الا ان الاحکام الخ غرض جواب سوال مقدر۔ سوال یہ ہے کہ جب قرآن کے دو معنی آتے ہیں اور اصل قرآن وہی کلام کلام لفظی پر کرتے ہیں اور اس سے بحث کرتے ہیں اور اسی بناء پر انہوں نے قرآن مجید کی تعریف میں ایسی صفات کا اعتبار کیا جو کلام لفظی کو کلام ازلی سے امتیاز دیدیں اس بناء پر اس تعریف کو تشخیص کہا جائیگا۔ لا یقال اشکال یہ ہوا کہ اگر مقصود کلام لفظی کو کلام ازلی سے امتیاز دینا تھا تو امتیاز تو حد میں ما نقل کے ذکر کرتے ہے بھی حاصل ہو جاتا ہے کیونکہ کلام ازلی میں نقل نہیں ہو سکتی تو پھر دوسر کی صفات بین جاتا تھا لیکن مساوات نہیں حاصل ہوتی اسی مساوات کو حاصل کرنے کیلئے باقی قیودات کو ذکر کیا فلا اشکال
His statement 'rather its identification...' The intent of the commentator of Al-Tawdih is that the matn said: 'This is not a definition of the quiddity of the book, but rather an identification (tashkhis).' The commentator explains the meaning of tashkhis: Tashkhis means to distinguish a thing from others by mentioning its characteristics. When one asks about a thing using the interrogative word 'which', the purpose is to mention its characteristics and distinguish it, whether the thing is a person or non-person. His statement 'it applies to eternal speech...' The intent of the commentator is that the author of the matn said that this definition is identification, not a statement of quiddity. Then he gave the evidence for identification: that the word 'Qur'an' is applied to two meanings: sometimes it is applied to eternal speech (kalam azali) which is an attribute of Allah, and sometimes to this collection (majmu'ah) which people recite, called verbal speech (kalam lafzi). Therefore, this definition was made to identify one of these two. While explaining, the commentator says: The word 'Qur'an' applies to two meanings: (1) Eternal speech (kalam azali), also called internal speech (kalam nafsi). It is eternal and an attribute of Allah. The proof for this is the prophetic hadith: 'The Qur'an is the speech of Allah, uncreated' (some scholars consider this a fabricated hadith; it is not his saying but the saying of the Salaf). This establishes that the Noble Qur'an is applied to the speech of Allah. He explained the eternal speech: It is an eternal attribute, opposite to silence and defect; it is not of the genus of letters and sounds because these are all created; nor is it a command or prohibition; nor does it relate to past, present, or future. However, in terms of relations (muta'alliqat) and additions (idafat), it relates to past, present, and future, just like other attributes such as knowledge and power relate to past etc. by way of relations. The second meaning of Qur'an is 'that which is recited upon the tongues of the servants.' Elucidating this, the commentator says: This is verbal speech, created, composed of sounds and letters, subsisting in its place (mahall). It is also called the speech of Allah and named 'Qur'an' in the sense that the original speech of Allah and Qur'an is that which is the eternal attribute, and this verbal speech indicates and expresses it. 'Except that the rulings...' The intent is to answer an anticipated question: When there are two meanings of Qur'an and the original Qur'an is the eternal speech, why do the usulis not discuss that and instead discuss the verbal speech calling it Qur'an? The commentator replies: The reason is that the usuli's purpose is related to this verbal speech because the rulings (ahkam) are attached and dependent on this verbal speech, not on the internal speech (kalam nafsi). The usulis consider the speech of Allah in terms of it being the establisher of rulings. Therefore, they apply the term 'Noble Qur'an' to verbal speech and discuss it. On this basis, they have included in the definition of the Noble Qur'an such attributes that distinguish verbal speech from eternal speech. Due to this, the definition is called identification (tashkhis). It cannot be said that if the purpose was to distinguish verbal speech from eternal speech, the distinction would be achieved by mentioning 'that which is transmitted (manqul)' because eternal speech cannot be transmitted, so mentioning the other attributes like 'between the two covers' is superfluous. The answer is that two things are necessary in a definition: first, it must distinguish the defined from everything else; second, the definition must be equivalent (musawi) to the defined. Now, 'transmitted' achieves distinction but not equivalence. To achieve equivalence, the remaining restrictions (quyud) were mentioned, so there is no objection.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage explains why the definition of the Qur'an is an identification (tashkhis) rather than a definition of quiddity (mahiyyah). The commentator clarifies that the word 'Qur'an' has two meanings: the eternal speech (kalam azali/nafsi) which is an attribute of Allah, and the recited collection (kalam lafzi) which is created and composed of letters and sounds. The usuli is concerned with the latter because legal rulings (ahkam) are derived from the recited words, not from the eternal attribute. Therefore, the definition of the Qur'an as 'the book between the two covers' (bayna al-daffatayn) is meant to identify the recited Qur'an and distinguish it from the eternal speech. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if the goal is to distinguish from eternal speech, why mention 'between the two covers' in addition to 'that which is transmitted'? Because 'transmitted' alone distinguishes from eternal speech (which cannot be transmitted) but does not achieve equivalence (musawah) of the definition to the defined. The recited Qur'an has other properties (like being written in a Mushaf) that are essential to its complete definition. Thus, additional restrictions are needed to fully capture its essence and exclude anything else (like hadith or other revealed books). Hence, the definition is an identification that specifies the individual instance (majmu' shakhsi) of the recited Qur'an.

Key Terms
تشخیص (tashkhis): Identification; mentioning characteristics to distinguish a thing from others.
کلام ازلی (kalam azali): Eternal speech, an attribute of Allah, uncreated, not composed of letters/sounds.
کلام لفظی (kalam lafzi): Verbal speech, created, composed of letters and sounds, subsisting in its place.
مساوات (musawah): Equivalence; the definition must be coextensive with the defined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do usulis focus on the verbal speech (kalam lafzi) rather than the eternal speech?
A) Because the verbal speech is older.
B) Because legal rulings depend on the verbal speech, not the eternal speech.
C) Because the eternal speech is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the verbal speech is easier to understand.

Key Takeaway
The definition of the Qur'an in Hanafi usul is an identification (tashkhis) aimed at the verbal speech, not the eternal attribute, and must be coextensive with the defined.

Al-Quran as Source

The Quran is Divine Words and Meaning: A Hanafi Definition
Clarifying that Quran is both words and meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the Quran as nazm indicating meaning and explain why the definition excludes both pure words and pure meaning.

Core Idea
The Quran is a divinely ordered Arabic text (nazm) that indicates meaning; neither mere words nor mere meaning alone qualify.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi usul definition of the Quran. A common misunderstanding arises from a ruling by Imam Abu Hanifa that seems to prioritize meaning over words in prayer. By carefully analyzing the text and its context, we will see that the Quran is defined as a nazm (structured text) that inherently carries meaning, and that the prayer ruling was a specific concession later retracted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Note the two key terms: 'nazm' (text/composition) and 'lafz' (word/utterance). The author deliberately chooses 'nazm' for the Quran, not 'lafz', because nazm carries connotations of order and etiquette while lafz is bare utterance.
This choice is significant: it elevates the Quran's structure as part of its definition.
2
Understand why nazm is preferred
The author explains that 'nazm' means stringing pearls, implying a deliberate arrangement. The Quran is not random words but a divinely arranged text. Using 'lafz' would be disrespectful.
This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on the Quran's inimitability and divine order.
3
Address the misconception from Abu Hanifa's prayer ruling
Some thought that since Abu Hanifa allowed Persian recitation in prayer, he regarded the Quran as mere meaning. The author clarifies that this ruling was a specific concession based on ease (tawsi'ah) and was later retracted.
This shows that a narrow ruling should not be generalized to define the Quran itself.
4
Recognize the scope and retraction
Outside prayer, even Abu Hanifa required Arabic words. Moreover, the sound opinion is that he retracted the prayer concession, aligning with the majority.
Thus the definition remains: Quran = Arabic words + meaning.
5
Derive the full definition
Combine the points: Quran is the Arabic nazm (revealed words) that indicates meaning. It is not mere words (lafz) without meaning, nor mere meaning without Arabic wording.
This definition has practical implications: recitation in prayer must be in Arabic, and translations are not Quran.

Since the Quran is a text (nazm) indicating meaning, the word is divided into four divisions in relation to meaning. The purpose is that the Quran is the name of a text that indicates meaning, not mere text as might be suspected from the definition, because 'revealed, written, transmitted' are all attributes of the word, not meaning. And it is not the name of mere meaning, as might be suspected from the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa that reciting the words of the Quran in prayer is not necessary; if someone recites in Persian in prayer it is permissible. So the author clarifies that the Quran is the name of words in which meaning is considered. Therefore the upcoming four divisions relate to the words of the Quran but also consider meaning. The intended meaning of 'nazm' here is 'word' (lafz). The lexical meaning of 'lafz' is 'to throw' and the lexical meaning of 'nazm' is 'stringing pearls on a thread'. Because 'nazm' contains good etiquette and the meaning of 'lafz' has some bad etiquette, the term 'nazm' is applied to the words of the Quran, not 'lafz'. It has been narrated from Abu Hanifa: a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that recitation in non-Arabic in prayer is permissible; if someone performs the meaning in Persian, his prayer will be valid. This narration gave the false impression that perhaps the Quran is the name of meaning and text and wording have no role. Therefore the author clarifies that according to Imam Abu Hanifa also the Quran is the name of both words and meaning, but specifically in prayer Imam Abu Hanifa did not make recitation of the exact words an essential pillar; rather he said that if someone without excuse performs the meaning in his own language or Persian, his prayer and the pillar of recitation will be performed because expansion and facility in the words of the Quran were considered. This is why the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) allowed different tribes to recite the Quran in their own dialect. On this basis, Imam Abu Hanifa did not consider the words necessary specifically in prayer. However, outside of prayer, Imam Abu Hanifa also required the words, as in the recitation of a jinni or a menstruating woman: if they only perform the meaning without reciting the words, it is not permissible for them because the meaning is not truly Quran unless accompanied by Arabic words. For this reason the author mentioned the word 'specific' (khaassatan) that this statement of Imam Abu Hanifa is only and exclusively about prayer. But the sound opinion is that he retracted this statement. The author says: Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his statement and made the words necessary even in prayer, like the majority opinion. This is why in the text of al-Tanqih I did not mention this statement at all, but I...
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage provides a precise definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author (Sadr al-Shariah in Al-Tawdih or the commentator) states that the Quran is a 'nazm' (text/verbal composition) that indicates meaning. This definition deliberately avoids two extremes: that the Quran is only words (lafz) with no regard for meaning, or that it is only meaning independent of words. The term 'nazm' is chosen over 'lafz' because it carries a connotation of good order and etiquette. The section then addresses a potential misunderstanding arising from a well-known narration of Imam Abu Hanifa that allowed recitation in Persian in prayer, which might suggest that the Quran is only meaning. The commentator clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling was specific to prayer and based on the principle of ease (tawsi'ah), as the Prophet permitted different dialects. However, outside prayer, even Imam Abu Hanifa required Arabic words. Moreover, the sound opinion (al-ash) is that Imam Abu Hanifa later retracted this view, aligning with the majority. Therefore, the definition of Quran includes both Arabic words and their meanings. This is crucial for understanding that recitation in prayer (qira'ah) must be in Arabic, and that non-Arabic translations are not considered Quran.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): Verbal composition/text with order and meaning; specifically the Quranic text as a structured whole.
لفظ (lafz): Word or utterance; in this context, the mere phonetic form without consideration of meaning or structure.
قرآن (Quran): The divinely revealed Arabic text (nazm) indicating meaning; both words and meaning are integral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct definition of the Quran?
A) Only the meanings of the revealed words.
B) Only the Arabic words without consideration of meaning.
C) A text (nazm) that indicates meaning, comprising both words and meaning.
D) Any composition in Arabic that contains religious teachings.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the Arabic nazm that conveys meaning; both elements are essential and inseparable in Hanafi usul.

Al-Quran as Source
intermediate
8 min
Definition of the Quran and Fourfold Division of Words in Hanafi Usul
Defining the Quran as nazm dall ala al-ma'na and introducing four divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of the Quran as 'nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na', explain its significance, and identify the four divisions of words according to Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The Quran is a composition that carries meaning – both words and meaning are essential; Hanafi usul divides words into four precise categories for istinbat.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the precise Hanafi definition of the Quran by addressing a common misunderstanding about Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation. He then introduces the fourfold division of words, which is the backbone of Hanafi usul analysis. By mastering this definition and its implied categories, you will be able to navigate how Hanafi jurists derive rulings from the Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition: nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na
Break down the Arabic: nazm (composition) that indicates meaning. The Quran is not mere speech; it is structured words that convey specific meanings.
Note the choice of 'nazm' over 'lafz' for its positive connotation.
2
Resolve the apparent contradiction: Imam Abu Hanifa's Persian ruling
The commentator addresses the potential misunderstanding that the Quran is only meaning because Imam Abu Hanifa allowed Persian recitation in prayer. He explains that this was a special dispensation for prayer, later retracted, and does not negate the integral role of the Arabic words.
The ruling was specific to prayer and ease for non-Arab converts.
3
Map the four divisions of words
Identify each division: (1) by wad' (linguistic assignment) – khass, 'amm, mushtarak; (2) by isti'mal (usage) – haqiqah, majaz; (3) by zuhur/khafa' (clarity/obscurity) – zahir, nass, mujmal, mutashabih; (4) by kayfiyat al-dalala (quality of indication) – 'ibarah, isharah, dalalah, iqtida'.
The author ordered them differently from Fakhr al-Islam because usage precedes clarity.
4
Apply the framework to a Quranic example
Take the command 'Aqimu al-salah' and analyze it through the four divisions. This demonstrates how the Hanafi usul method works in practice.
This prepares you for later lessons on each division.

هو النظم والمعنى فالظاهر ان مرادهم النظم الدال على المعنى فاخترت هذه العبارة ، مصنف فرما رہے ہیں کہ ہمارے مشائخ نے کہا ہے کہ القرآن هو النظم والمعنی کہ قرآن مجید نظم اور معنی دونوں کے مجموعے کا نام ہے اس سے یہ نہ سمجھا جائے کہ قرآن مجید میں نظم اور معنی دو الگ الگ چیزیں ہیں اور قرآن پاک جس طرح حقیقتا لفظ کی صفت ہے اسی طرح معنی کی بھی صفت ہے بلکہ ان حضرات کی مراد بھی یہی ہے کہ قرآن مجید نام وصفت ہے نظم کی البتہ اس نظم میں معنی کا لحاظ بھی رکھا گیا ہے اس کو تعبیر کیا گیا۔ اعظم الدال علی المعنی کے ساتھ مصنف فرمارہے ہیں اسی وجہ سے میں نے باقی تمام عبادات کو چھوڑ کر اعظم الدال علی المعنی والی عبارت کو اختیار کیا ہے خلاصہ یہ کہ آگے جتنی تقسیمات آ رہی ہیں ان سب کا تعلق نظم قرآن کے ساتھ ہے لیکن بالنسبة الى المعنی فقط نظم کے ساتھ بھی نہیں ہے فقط معنی کے ساتھ بھی نہیں ہے۔ باعتبار وضعہ لہ یعنی پہلی تقسیم لفظ کی باعتبار وضع کے ہے اس اعتبار سے کلام کی چار قسمیں نہیں خاص عام مشترک ( مصنف نے ماڈل کو ذکر نہیں کیا وجہ آگے آئے گی ) اس تقسیم کو فخر الاسلام نے الاول فی وجوہ النظم صیغہ ولغۃ ( یعنی پہلی تقسیم صیغہ اور لغت کے اعتبار سے ہے) سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔ نشم باعتبار استعمالہ فیہ دوسری تقسیم استعمال کے اعتبار سے ہے کہ لفظ معنی موضوع لہ میں استعمال ہو رہا ہے یا غیر معنی موضوع لہ میں ثم باعتبار ظهور المعنی عنہ و خفا کو تیسری تقسیم ظہور معنی اور خفاء معنی اور ان کے مراتب کے اعتبار سے ہے اس کو فخر الاسلام نے والثانی فی وجوہ البیان بذالک النظم ( یعنی ثانی تقسیم بیان کے طرق میں ہے اس نظم کے ساتھ ) سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔ وانما جعلت هذا التقسيم ثالثا واعتبارا لاستعمال ثانيا على عكس ما اورده فخر الاسلام الخ مصنف سوال کا جواب دے رہے ہیں سوال یہ ہے کہ فخر الاسلام نے ظہور وفاء کو تقسیم ثانی اور استعمال کو تقسیم ثالث بنایا ہے آپ نے اس کے برعکس کیا ہے آپ نے فخر الاسلام کی مخالفت کیوں کی ہے؟ مصنف جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ چونکہ لفظ کی استعمال معنی میں پہلے ہوتی ہے اور ظہور معنی اور خفاء بعد میں ہوتا ہے اس لیے میں نے استعمال کو تقسیم ثانی اور ظہور وفاء و تقسیم ثالث قرار دیا ہے۔ ثم فی کیفیت دلالتہ علیہ یعنی چوتھی تقسیم لفظ کی دلالت کی کیفیت کے اعتبار سے ہے معنی پر اس کو فخر الاسلام نے الرابع في وجوه الوقوف علی احکام العظم ( یعنی چوتھی تقسیم نظم کے احکام پر واقفیت حاصل کرنے کے طریقوں میں ہے ) سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔
Since the Quran is a composition indicating meaning, the word is divided with respect to meaning into four divisions. The purpose is to clarify that the Quran is the name of the composition that indicates meaning; it is not merely empty composition as might be suspected from the definition because 'sent down', 'written', 'transmitted' are all attributes of the word, not meaning. Nor is it merely meaning, as is misunderstood from Imam Abu Hanifa's statement that reciting the words of the Quran in prayer is not obligatory; if one recites in Persian it is valid. So the author clarified that the Quran is the name of words in which meaning is considered. Thus the four divisions that follow pertain to the words of the Quran but also consider meaning. By 'nazm' here is meant 'lafz' (word); 'nazm' linguistically means stringing pearls, while 'lafz' means throwing. Since 'nazm' carries good etiquette and 'lafz' carries some bad etiquette, the term 'nazm' is applied to the words of the Quran, not 'lafz'. It has been narrated from Abu Hanifa that recitation in prayer with non-Arabic is permissible; if someone performs the meaning in Persian, his prayer is valid. From this narration it was understood that perhaps the Quran is only meaning, with no role for composition and expression. So the author clarifies that according to Imam Abu Hanifa too, the Quran is the name of both words and meaning, but specifically in prayer he did not make recitation of Arabic words an obligatory pillar. He said that if someone without excuse performs the meaning in his own language or Persian, his prayer and the pillar of recitation are fulfilled because expansion and ease were considered for the words of the Quran. This is why the Prophet allowed different tribes to recite the Quran in their own language. On this basis, Imam Abu Hanifa did not make the words obligatory specifically in prayer. However, outside prayer, he made words obligatory, as in the recitation of a jinni or a menstruating woman: if they only perform the meaning without reciting the words, it is not permissible because meaning is not truly Quran without the Arabic words. Hence the author used the word 'specifically' to indicate that Imam's statement is only about prayer. But the correct view is that he retracted from this statement; the author says that Imam retracted and made words necessary in prayer as well, like the majority. That is why the author did not mention this statement at all in the text of al-Tanqih; rather he said that the Quran is the name of nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na. And our scholars said that the Quran is al-nazm and al-ma'na; the apparent meaning is that they intended nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na, so I chose this expression. The author says that our scholars said 'al-Quran huwa al-nazm wa al-ma'na' meaning that the Quran is the name of the combination of both nazm and ma'na. From this it should not be understood that nazm and ma'na are two separate things and that the Quran is an attribute of meaning just as it is an attribute of word; rather their intention is that the Quran is the name and attribute of nazm, but in this nazm, meaning is taken into account. This is expressed along with 'nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na'. The author says that for this reason I chose the expression 'nazm dall 'ala al-ma'na' leaving aside other expressions. Summary: all the divisions that follow pertain to the nazm of the Quran, but with respect to meaning, not solely nazm nor solely meaning. The first division is with respect to wad' (assignment); in this respect speech is divided into four categories: khass, 'amm, mushtarak (the author did not mention majaz; the reason will come later). This division is called by Fakhr al-Islam as 'the first with respect to the aspects of composed forms and language'. The second division is with respect to isti'mal (usage): whether the word is used in its assigned meaning or other than its assigned meaning. Then the third division is with respect to zuhur (clarity) and khafa' (obscurity) of meaning and their levels. This is called by Fakhr al-Islam as 'the second with respect to the aspects of expression by that composition'. And the author made this division third and isti'mal second, contrary to what Fakhr al-Islam presented. The author answers the question: Because the usage of the word in meaning occurs first, while clarity and obscurity occur later, so he made usage the second division and clarity/obscurity the third. Then the fourth division is with respect to the quality of indication upon meaning. This is called by Fakhr al-Islam as 'the fourth with respect to the aspects of understanding the rulings of the composition'.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of the Quran from a Hanafi usul work. The author begins by stating that the Quran is a 'nazm' (composition) that indicates meaning, and from this arises four divisions of words with respect to meaning. The commentator then clarifies a subtle point: the Quran is not merely a composition without meaning, nor is it merely meaning without words. He addresses a potential misunderstanding from Imam Abu Hanifa's famous view that recitation in prayer can be in Persian. The commentator explains that this ruling was specific to prayer and that Imam Abu Hanifa later retracted it. The definition adopted is that the Quran is the nazm (word-structure) that indicates meaning, emphasizing that both wording and meaning are integral. The passage then introduces the four divisions of words: (1) by wad' (linguistic assignment), (2) by isti'mal (usage), (3) by zuhur and khafa' (clarity/obscurity), and (4) by kayfiyat al-dalala (quality of indication). The commentator explains why he ordered them differently from Fakhr al-Islam: because usage logically precedes clarity/obscurity.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): Here it means the composed words of the Quran, chosen over 'lafz' for its positive connotation.
دال على المعنى (dall 'ala al-ma'na): Indicating a meaning; the composition conveys a specific intended meaning.
تقسيمات أربعة (taqsimat arba'a): The fourfold division of words with respect to meaning: wad', isti'mal, zuhur/khafa', and kayfiyat al-dalala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul definition presented, what is the Quran?
A) Pure meaning without words
B) A nazm (composition) that indicates meaning
C) Only the Arabic words, not the meaning
D) Any divine book regardless of language

Key Takeaway
The Quran is a composition that indicates meaning – both words and meaning are integral, and Hanafi usul divides words into four categories to systematically derive rulings.

Al-Quran as Source

Reconciling Quran as Nazm: Definition and Proof
Reconciling definitions of Quran as nazm and ma'na with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of the Quran as al-nazm al-dal 'ala al-ma'na and identify the proofs that support this definition from the Quran's attributes and i'jaz.

Core Idea
The Quran is primarily the verbal composition (nazm) that indicates meaning; its miraculous eloquence proves this.

In this passage, Taftazani reconciles apparent contradictions in Hanafi definitions of the Quran. The author defines the Quran as 'nazm indicating meaning', while some mashayikh say 'both nazm and ma'na'. Taftazani argues both are equivalent once we understand that the Quran is essentially the composition, with meaning being an inseparable but secondary component. He supports this by noting that all attributes of the Quran—being revealed, written, transmitted—are attributes of the wording, not of meaning alone. Furthermore, the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) is a property of its balaghah (eloquence), which is a quality of the wording. This lesson will walk you through the reconciliation and the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author (Sadr al-Shari'ah) defines the Quran as 'nazm indicating meaning'. The mashayikh say 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an'. Note the apparent difference: one seems to prioritize nazm, the other gives equal weight to ma'na.
This contradiction arises from different wordings, not from different legal positions.
2
Apply the reconciliation principle
Taftazani explains that the mashayikh's habit is to focus on intended meanings, not literal wordings. Their phrase 'nazm and ma'na together' actually means 'nazm indicating meaning', because they wanted to reject the view that the Quran is only meaning. Thus, both definitions are identical in substance.
Understanding this principle prevents false ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the proof from attributes
The Quran's definitional attributes—being revealed (munazzal), written in mushafs (maktub fi al-masahif), and transmitted mutawatir—are attributes of the nazm (wording), not of the meaning. For example, a meaning cannot be 'revealed' or 'written'; only words can. This proves that the Quran is essentially the nazm.
This is a strong dalil that the Quran is primarily the verbal composition.
4
Examine the proof from i'jaz
I'jaz (inimitability) is linked to balaghah (eloquence), which is a property of the wording (lafz). Balaghah is achieved when wording is chosen to suit context and purpose—for example, emphasis for a denier, or word order for importance. When balaghah reaches its peak, the speech becomes miraculous. Therefore, i'jaz is an attribute of nazm, not of meaning. This confirms that the Quran is defined as nazm, with meaning being a necessary consideration.
This argument also shows that meaning is always tied to nazm; it is not independent.

ہے وہ ہے الدال بطريق العبارة الدال بطريق الاشارة الدال بطريق الدلالته الدال بطریق الاقتضاء ، اور دلالت کرنے والا لفظ ہی ہوتا ہے لہذا ان اقسام کا تعلق بھی نظم کے ساتھ ہو سکتا ہے فلا تعارض بینھما و عدم الالتفات الى العبارات واختلافها من باب داب المشائخ غرض جواب سوال مقدر ! جب تمام تعبیرات کا حاصل ایک ہے تو علامہ فخر الاسلام نے مختلف انداز اور مختلف الفاظ سے تقسیم رابع کو کیوں ذکر کیا تو علامہ تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ ہما رے مشائخ کی عادت مبارکہ ہے کہ وہ الفاظ کی طرف توجہ نہیں کرتے عبارات کو پیش نظر نہیں رکھتے بلکہ ان کے مد نظر مقصود ہوتا ہے اس مقصود کے تعبیر کرنے میں بھی الفاظ کا اختلاف ہو جاتا ہے تو یہ قابل گرفت بات نہیں ہے۔ وعلى ما ذكره من تقسيم اللفظ بالنسبة الى المعنى الخ غرض تفتازانی جواب سوال ۔ سوال ! علامہ تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ کوئی تعارض نہیں ہے کیونکہ مشائخ کی عبارت کا مفہوم بھی بعینہ وہی ہے جو مصنف کی عبارت کا ہے یعنی نظم دال علی معنی کی تقسیم جس طرح قرآن پاک کے بارے میں ان مشائخ کا قول ہے القرآن والنظم والمعنی جمیعا اور مراد یہی ہے کہ النظم الدال على المعنی الہذا دونوں عبارتوں میں کوئی تعارض نہیں ہے اور حقیقت یہی ہے کہ قرآن پاک نظم اور الفاظ کا نام ہے البتہ اس میں معنی لحاظ کیا گیا ہے۔ للقطع بان كونه عربيا مكتوبا في المصاحف الخ اس عبارت سے تفتازانی گذشته دعوی پر دلیل قائم کر رہے ہیں کہ قرآن مجید نام ہے نظم کا بالنسبۃ الی المعنی کیونکہ قرآن مجید کی تعریف میں جو صفات ذکر گئی المنزل المكتوب في المصاحف المنقول عند نقل متواتر ابلا شبہ ، یہ سب حقیقتہ نظم کی صفات ہیں بالنسبتہ الی المعنی نہ کر نظم اور معنی دونوں کی۔ وكذا الاعجاز يتعلق بالبلاغة وهى من الصفات الراجعة الى اللفظ باعتبار أفادته المعنی الخ مقصد عبارت یہ ہے کہ اگر علامہ ابن الحاجب والی تعریف کی جائے القرآن هو الكلام المنزل للاعجاز بسورۃ منہ تو بھی اعجاز اصالت و حقیقہ نظم کی صفات میں سے ہے کیونکہ اعجاز کا تعلق بلاغت کے ساتھ ہے اس لیے کہ اعجاز کا معنی ہے کلام کا اس حد پر پہنچ جانا کہ اس کا معارضہ ممکن نہ ہو اور بلاغت الفاظ کی صفات میں سے ہے کیونکہ اگر آپ کسی ترکیب و کلام کے ساتھ کوئی معنی اداء کرنا چاہتے ہیں تو وہاں مختلف اغراض و مقاصد پیدا ہو جاتے ہیں مثلا بھی غرض کی پوری تحقیر ہوتی ہے کبھی تعظیم کبھی اظہار بلادت کبھی اظہار ذکاوت یہ اغراض مختلفہ تقاضا کرتی ہیں نظم میں مختلف کیفیات و خصوصیات کا مثلا اگر مخاطب منکر ہے تو انکار مخاطب تقاضہ کرتا ہے کلام میں تاکید ہو اسی طرح حضر مقصود ہو تو وہ تقاضہ کرتی ہے کلام میں تقدیم و تاخیر کا اگر ان خصوصیات و کیفیات کا بحسب الاغراض بقدر طاقت بشریہ اعتبار کر کے کلام کو ذکر کیا جائے تو اس کو کلام بلیغ کہا جاتا ہے پھر اگر وہ کلام بلاغت کی اس حد پر پہنچ جائے کہ اس کا معارضہ ممکن نہ ہو تو ایسی کلام کو معجز کہا جاتا ہے اس سے ثابت ہوا کہ اعجاز بھی بالذابت و بالا صالہ نظم کی صفت ہے نہ کہ نظم اور معنی دونوں کی البتہ اس میں معنی کا لحاظ ضرور ہوتا ہے تو ثابت ہوا کہ مصنف کا عنوان اقسام النظم الدال علی المعنی ہی انسب واوفق بالصواب ہے۔
It is the one that indicates by way of expression, indication, implication, and requirement, and the indicator is only the word. Therefore these divisions can relate to composition (nazm) as well, so there is no contradiction between them, and ignoring expressions and their differences is from the habit of the mashayikh. Purpose: answer to an anticipated question: when all expressions have one outcome, why did Fakhr al-Islam mention the fourth division in a different style and words? Taftazani answers that our mashayikh have the blessed habit of not paying attention to words and expressions, rather they focus on the intended meaning, and in expressing that meaning, differences in words occur, so this is not objectionable. And based on what he mentioned of dividing the word in relation to meaning, etc. the purpose of Taftazani is to answer a question: it is that there is contradiction between the statements of the author and the mashayikh. The author said that the Quran is the name of the composition indicating the meaning, meaning that originally the Quran is the name of the words, but in it the relation to meaning is also considered. And the statement of the mashayikh is this: divisions of nazm and ma'na, meaning that these divisions relate to both nazm and ma'na. So there is contradiction. Answer: Taftazani says there is no contradiction because the meaning of the mashayikh's statement is exactly that of the author's statement, i.e., division of nazm indicating meaning, just as their statement about the Quran is 'al-Quran wal-nazm wal-ma'na jami'an', and the intent is that nazm indicates meaning. So there is no contradiction, and in reality the Quran is the name of nazm and words, but meaning is considered in it. For the definitive proof that it is Arabic, written in the musahif, etc. With this expression, Taftazani establishes proof for his previous claim that the Quran is the name of nazm in relation to meaning, because the attributes mentioned in the definition of the Quran - al-munazzal, al-maktub fi al-masahif, al-manqul bil-naql al-mutawatir bila shubbah - all are in reality attributes of nazm in relation to meaning, not of both nazm and meaning. Likewise, i'jaz (inimitability) is related to balaghah (eloquence), which is an attribute returning to the word by virtue of its conveying meaning. The purpose is that if the definition of Ibn al-Hajib is taken - al-Quran huwa al-kalam al-munazzal lil-i'jaz bi suratin minhu - then i'jaz is also essentially a property of nazm, because i'jaz is related to balaghah, because i'jaz means the speech reaching a level where it cannot be opposed, and balaghah is among the attributes of words, because if you intend to convey a meaning with a composition/speech, various purposes and aims arise. For example, sometimes the purpose is belittlement, sometimes glorification, sometimes showing ignorance, sometimes showing intelligence. These different aims demand different qualities and characteristics in nazm. For example, if the addressee is a denier, the denial of the addressee requires emphasis in speech; similarly, if presence is intended, it requires precedence and delay. If these qualities and characteristics are considered in the speech according to the purposes to the extent of human ability, then it is called eloquent speech. Then if that speech reaches such a level of eloquence that it cannot be opposed, such speech is called miraculous. From this it is proven that i'jaz is essentially a property of nazm, not of both nazm and meaning, although meaning is necessarily considered in it. Thus it is proven that the author's title 'divisions of nazm indicating meaning' is more appropriate and correct.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and reconciliation of different scholarly expressions regarding the definition of the Quran. The commentator, Taftazani, is addressing two apparent contradictions. First, why did Fakhr al-Islam use different wording for the fourth division? The answer is that the mashayikh (early Hanafi scholars) did not fixate on particular wording; they focused on the intended meaning. Therefore, variations in expression do not matter if the underlying meaning is the same. Second, there appears to be a contradiction between the author (Musannif, i.e., Sadr al-Shari'ah) who defines Quran as 'nazm dal ala al-ma'na' (composition indicating meaning) and the mashayikh who say 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' (both composition and meaning). Taftazani argues that both expressions are equivalent: the mashayikh's phrase means the composition that indicates meaning. To prove that Quran is essentially nazm (the verbal composition) and not meaning alone, Taftazani points out that all attributes of the Quran—such as being revealed, written in mushafs, transmitted mutawatir—are attributes of the nazm, not of the meaning. Similarly, the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Quran pertains to its eloquence (balaghah), which is a property of the wording. Eloquence is achieved by choosing appropriate wording to suit the context (e.g., emphasis for a denier, precedence for something important). When eloquence reaches an unsurpassable level, it becomes i'jaz. Thus i'jaz is an attribute of nazm, though always in relation to meaning. Therefore, the author's title 'aqsam al-nazm al-dal 'ala al-ma'na' is the most appropriate.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The composition or arrangement of words; the verbal form of speech.
معنى (ma'na): The meaning conveyed by the nazm.
إعجاز (i'jaz): The miraculous nature of the Quran that renders it inimitable.
بلاغة (balaghah): Eloquence; speech that suits the context and conveys meaning effectively.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, why do the mashayikh use the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an'?
A) Because they consider Quran to be both the words and the meaning equally.
B) Because they want to emphasize that Quran is the nazm indicating meaning, to counter the notion that it is only meaning.
C) Because they disagree with the author's definition.
D) Because they think meaning is more important than words.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is defined as the verbal composition (nazm) that indicates meaning; this is proven by the Quran's attributes and its miraculous eloquence.

Al-Quran as Source

Debate on the Locus of I'jaz: Nazm vs. Ma'na and the Mashayikh's Expression
Presenting second opinion on i'jaz as property of meaning and refuting it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the dispute on whether i'jaz is a property of nazm or ma'na, explain the Hanafi position with Taftazani's refutation, and apply the mashayikh's phrase to counter a common misconception.

Core Idea
I'jaz (inimitability) resides in the Quran's unique wording (nazm), not in meaning alone.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary addresses a key dispute in Usul al-Fiqh: is the Quran's miraculous nature (i'jaz) a property of its wording or its meaning? The Hanafi school holds that i'jaz is a property of the nazm. A second opinion attributes i'jaz to the meaning alone. Taftazani refutes this second view and explains why the early mashayikh used the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' to counter a potential misinterpretation of Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on recitation in Persian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions on i'jaz
State the first position (i'jaz is of nazm - Hanafi) and the second position (i'jaz is of meaning alone).
The second opinion is not attributed to a specific scholar but is mentioned by Taftazani.
2
Examine the proof for the second opinion
The second opinion argues that the deep meanings of verses like Surah al-Fatihah are beyond human capacity, citing the saying of Ali (ra) that the Quran is an inexhaustible sea.
3
Analyze Taftazani's refutation
Taftazani responds that the abundance of meaning is actually a property of the unique wording (nazm) which can convey such depth. Other words cannot achieve this, so i'jaz returns to nazm.
The key is that the meaning is not independent; it is the nazm that contains the meaning.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If i'jaz were in meaning, a translation could be considered miraculous. But since i'jaz is in nazm, only the Arabic wording of the Quran is inimitable.
Also relevant to the ruling on recitation in Persian.
5
Explain the mashayikh's expression
The mashayikh said 'al-Quran huwa al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' to counter the mistaken idea that the Quran is just meaning (arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's permissibility of Persian recitation). Taftazani clarifies that they meant nazm indicating meaning.
This shows the importance of precise expressions to avoid misinterpretation.

وقد يقال ان معنى القرآن نفسه معجز یہاں سے تفتازانی صفت اعجاز کے بارے میں دوسرا قول بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ بعض حضرات کے نزدیک اعجاز کلام معنی قرآن کی صفت ہے یعنی فقط معنی قرآن مجید بھی منجر ہے کیونکہ قرآن پاک کے الفاظ کے معانی اس قدر کثیر ہیں کہ ان پر اطلاع حاصل کرنا طاقت بشر سے باہر ہے جیسا کہ مشہور ہے کہ سورۃ فاتحہ کی تفسیر و معانی و مطالب اس قدر کثیر ہیں کہ اگر اس کو لکھا جائے تو کئی اونٹوں کے بوجھ بن سکتے ہیں اسی طرح حضرت علی کا مقولہ مشہور ہے القرآن بحر لا تنقضی عجائبہ کہ قرآن مجید ایک سمندر ہے جس کے عجائبات ختم نہیں ہو سکتے اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ معنی قرآن مجید بھی معجز ہے۔ جواب اسے شارح تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہ بھی در حقیقت نظم کا ہی اعجاز ہے کیونکہ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ الفاظ قرآن پاک اتنے کثیر معانی و مطالب پر مشتمل ہیں کہ دیگر الفاظ وعبارات اتنے معافی پر مشتمل نہیں ہو سکتے اس لیے قول ثانی ضعیف ہے۔ و مقصود المشائخ من قولهم هو النظم والمعنى جميعا دفع لتوهم الناشي غرض شارح تفتازانی سوال مقدر کا جواب سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب مشائخ کی عبارت القرآن هو العظم والعنی جمیعا کا مطلب نظم دال علی المعنی ہے تو مشائخ نے یہ تعبیر کیوں اختیار کی جس سے شبہ ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن پاک بالذات و بالا صالہ نظم کی صفت بھی ہے اور معنی کی بھی ان کو چاہئے تھا کہ نظم دال علی المعنی والا عنوان اختیار کرتے علامہ تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ مشائخ نے یہ عنوان اس وہم وشبہ کو زائل کرنے کیلئے اختیار کیا ہے جو امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول نماز میں قرآة بالفارسیہ کے جواز سے پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن پاک صرف معنی کا نام ہے نظم کا اس سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے اس لیے اس وہم کو دور کرنے کیلئے مشائخ نے جو انظلم والمعنی کہند یا مردوان کی بھی نظم دال علی معنی ہے۔ قوله المراد بالمنظم مهذا اللفظ لا يقال النظم على ما فسره المعتقون الخ غرض تفتازانی جواب سوال مقدر۔ سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ مصنف نے یہ فرمایا تھا کہ یہاں نظم تے مراد لفظ ہے جس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ نظم اور لفظ آپس میں مترادف ہیں ان کا معنی ایک ہے اور آگے جو اقسام آ رہی ہیں خاص عام مشترک وغیرہ جس طرح یہ لفظ کی قسمیں بن سکتی ہیں اسی طرح نظم کی بھی بن سکتی ہیں حالانکہ یہ تمام باتیں درست نہیں نظم اور لفظ آپس میں مترادف
And it is said that the meaning of the Quran itself is miraculous. From here Taftazani mentions the second opinion regarding the attribute of i'jaz: according to some, i'jaz is an attribute of the meaning of the Quran. That is, just the meaning of the Quran is also miraculous because the meanings of the words of the Quran are so numerous that it is beyond human power to comprehend them. As it is famous that the exegesis and meanings and themes of Surah al-Fatihah are so extensive that if they were written, they would form loads of several camels. Similarly, the saying of Ali (ra) is famous: 'al-Qur'anu bahrun la tanqadi ajaibuhu' meaning the Quran is a sea whose wonders never end. From this it is understood that the meaning of the Quran is also miraculous. Answer: the commentator Taftazani replies that this is also actually i'jaz of the nazm, because its meaning is that the words of the Quran contain so many meanings and themes that other words and expressions cannot contain that many meanings. Therefore the second opinion is weak. And the purpose of the mashayikh's statement 'huwa al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' is to remove the misconception that arose. The purpose of the commentator Taftazani is to answer an anticipated question: when the mashayikh's statement 'al-Quran huwa al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' means nazm indicating meaning, why did they choose this expression, which suggests that the Quran is essentially a property of both nazm and meaning? They should have chosen the title 'nazm dal 'ala al-ma'na'. Taftazani answers that the mashayikh chose this title to remove the misconception that arises from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on the permissibility of recitation in Persian in prayer, that the Quran is only the name of meaning and has no connection to nazm. So to remove this misconception, the mashayikh said 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na' meaning that it is nazm indicating meaning. His saying: 'al-murad bil-munazam min hadha al-lafz la yuqal al-nazm 'ala ma fassarahu al-mu'taqun' etc. The purpose of Taftazani is to answer an anticipated question: the author said that here nazm means lafz (word), which suggests that nazm and lafz are synonymous with one meaning, and the upcoming divisions like khas, 'amm, mushtarak etc. can be divisions of both lafz and nazm. However, these statements are not correct; nazm and lafz are synonymous.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the nature of the Quran, specifically addressing a second opinion on i'jaz. Some scholars argued that the meaning of the Quran itself is miraculous, pointing to the profound depth of meanings in even a short surah like al-Fatihah. They cite the famous saying of Ali (ra) that the Quran is an inexhaustible sea. Taftazani refutes this, arguing that this depth of meaning is not a property of meaning alone but rather of the nazm (wording) that encompasses these meanings. Human speech cannot carry such depth; it is the specific wording of the Quran that enables this. Thus i'jaz remains a property of nazm, not independent meaning. Taftazani then explains why the mashayikh used the phrase 'al-nazm wa al-ma'na jami'an' rather than simply 'nazm dal 'ala al-ma'na'. The reason is to counter a misconception arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling that reciting the Quran in Persian in prayer is permissible. Some might infer from this that the Quran is merely meaning, independent of its Arabic wording. The mashayikh's phrase emphasizes that Quran is both nazm and meaning, but in the sense that the nazm indicates the meaning. Finally, Taftazani addresses a question about the author's equation of nazm and lafz, clarifying that they are not synonymous in every context, but the discussion continues.

Key Terms
اعجاز (i'jaz): The miraculous, inimitable nature of the Quran.
معنى (ma'na): The meaning or content of the Quranic words.
نظم (nazm): The specific Arabic composition of the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why does the second opinion (that i'jaz is a property of meaning) ultimately support the first opinion (that i'jaz is of nazm)?
A) Because the second opinion is baseless and has no evidence.
B) Because the depth of meaning is actually a property of the nazm that contains it.
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa explicitly said so.
D) Because the Quran is purely nazm without meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the inimitability (i'jaz) of the Quran resides in its Arabic wording (nazm), even though the meanings are profound; this is essential for understanding the Quran's linguistic miracle and the ruling on recitation in other languages.

I'jaz: In Nazm or Meaning? The Hanafi Position
Dispute about whether inimitability is in nazm or meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on i'jaz and explain why it is attributed to nazm rather than meaning alone, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The Qur'an's i'jaz lies in its nazm (composition), which conveys the meaning; meaning alone is not the source of inimitability.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents and refutes the view that i'jaz is solely in the meaning. He argues that even the vastness of meaning is a function of the unique nazm. He also explains why the mashayikh phrased the definition of the Qur'an as 'nazm and meaning together'—to preempt a misunderstanding arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation. Let us unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
One view holds that i'jaz is an attribute of the meaning; the Hanafi view holds it is in nazm (composition).
Both agree the Qur'an is miraculous, but differ on the locus.
2
Examine the supporting evidence for each
Opponents cite examples like the vast meanings of Surah al-Fatiha and Ali's saying. The Hanafi response: these examples actually prove i'jaz of nazm.
The opponent's evidence is turned to support the Hanafi position.
3
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
Taftazani argues that the meanings' vastness is due to the words; other words cannot convey the same. Hence, i'jaz is in nazm.
The rebuttal hinges on the inability of alternative wording to match the Qur'an.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If i'jaz were in meaning alone, non-Arabic recitation might be acceptable. But Hanafis require Arabic wording as part of the Qur'an's definition.
This affects rulings on recitation in prayer (e.g., Persian vs. Arabic).

وقد يقال ان معنى القرآن نفسه معجز یہاں سے تفتازانی صفت اعجاز کے بارے میں دوسرا قول بیان کر رہے ہیں کہ بعض حضرات کے نزدیک اعجاز کلام معنی قرآن کی صفت ہے یعنی فقط معنی قرآن مجید بھی منجر ہے کیونکہ قرآن پاک کے الفاظ کے معانی اس قدر کثیر ہیں کہ ان پر اطلاع حاصل کرنا طاقت بشر سے باہر ہے جیسا کہ مشہور ہے کہ سورۃ فاتحہ کی تفسیر و معانی و مطالب اس قدر کثیر ہیں کہ اگر اس کو لکھا جائے تو کئی اونٹوں کے بوجھ بن سکتے ہیں اسی طرح حضرت علی کا مقولہ مشہور ہے القرآن بحر لا تنقضی عجائبہ کہ قرآن مجید ایک سمندر ہے جس کے عجائبات ختم نہیں ہو سکتے اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ معنی قرآن مجید بھی معجز ہے۔ جواب اسے شارح تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہ بھی در حقیقت نظم کا ہی اعجاز ہے کیونکہ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ الفاظ قرآن پاک اتنے کثیر معانی و مطالب پر مشتمل ہیں کہ دیگر الفاظ وعبارات اتنے معافی پر مشتمل نہیں ہو سکتے اس لیے قول ثانی ضعیف ہے۔ و مقصود المشائخ من قولهم هو النظم والمعنى جميعا دفع لتوهم الناشي غرض شارح تفتازانی سوال مقدر کا جواب سوال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ جب مشائخ کی عبارت القرآن هو العظم والعنی جمیعا کا مطلب نظم دال علی المعنی ہے تو مشائخ نے یہ تعبیر کیوں اختیار کی جس سے شبہ ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن پاک بالذات و بالا صالہ نظم کی صفت بھی ہے اور معنی کی بھی ان کو چاہئے تھا کہ نظم دال علی المعنی والا عنوان اختیار کرتے علامہ تفتازانی جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ مشائخ نے یہ عنوان اس وہم وشبہ کو زائل کرنے کیلئے اختیار کیا ہے جو امام ابو حنیفہ کے قول نماز میں قرآة بالفارسیہ کے جواز سے پیدا ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن پاک صرف معنی کا نام ہے نظم کا اس سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے اس لیے اس وہم کو دور کرنے کیلئے مشائخ نے جو انظلم والمعنی کہند یا مردوان کی بھی نظم دال علی معنی ہے۔
It may be said that the meaning of the Qur'an itself is miraculous. Here Taftazani is presenting the second opinion about the attribute of i'jaz: according to some, i'jaz is an attribute of the meaning of the Qur'an, i.e., only the meaning of the Qur'an is miraculous because the meanings of the words of the Qur'an are so numerous that knowing them is beyond human power. For example, it is well known that the interpretation and meanings of Surah al-Fatiha are so extensive that if written, they could form loads of many camels. Similarly, the famous saying of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) 'al-Qur'anu bahrun la tanqadi 'aja'ibuhu' (the Qur'an is a sea whose wonders never end) indicates that the meaning of the Qur'an is miraculous. The commentator Taftazani responds that this is actually the i'jaz of nazm because it means that the words of the Qur'an contain so many meanings that other words and phrases cannot contain such meanings, so the second opinion is weak. And the mashayikh's statement 'it is nazm and meaning together' is to dispel the misunderstanding arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling permitting Persian recitation in prayer, as that ruling might suggest that the Qur'an is only meaning with no connection to nazm. To remove this misunderstanding, the mashayikh said 'nazm and meaning' or 'nazm indicating meaning'.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the nature of i'jaz (inimitability) of the Qur'an. The text first states an opposing view: some scholars hold that i'jaz is an attribute of the meaning alone, not the wording. They support this with examples like the vast meanings of Surah al-Fatiha and the saying of Ali (RA). Taftazani refutes this by arguing that even this supposed i'jaz of meaning is actually due to the nazm (composition) because it is the words that convey those vast meanings; other words cannot produce the same effect. Thus the second opinion is weak. Then Taftazani addresses a potential objection: if the mashayikh's statement 'the Qur'an is nazm and meaning together' implies that i'jaz belongs to both, why did they not use the phrase 'nazm indicating meaning'? He responds that their phrasing was intentional to counter the misunderstanding arising from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling allowing Persian recitation in prayer, which some took to mean the Qur'an is only meaning. To emphasize that nazm is integral, they explicitly said 'nazm and meaning'.

Key Terms
اعجاز (i'jaz): The miraculous quality of the Qur'an that cannot be imitated by humans.
نظم (nazm): The composition of words in a meaningful, ordered structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the mashayikh say 'the Qur'an is nazm and meaning together' instead of 'nazm indicating meaning'?
A) To show that i'jaz is in both nazm and meaning equally.
B) To counter the misunderstanding from Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on Persian recitation.
C) Because they did not consider nazm important.
D) To emphasize that meaning alone is miraculous.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that the i'jaz of the Qur'an resides in its nazm (composition), not in meaning alone, and this has practical implications for the requirement of Arabic recitation.

Reciting in Persian in Prayer: The Usul Dispute
Explaining the usul dispute on reciting non-Arabic in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the usul principles (tawsi'ah and definition of Quran) that underpin the dispute on reciting non-Arabic in prayer, and evaluate the Hanafi arguments.

Core Idea
Is the Quran its words or its meaning? The Hanafi dispute turns on this usul question.

This passage presents a classic usul dispute: can a person capable of Arabic recite the Quran in Persian during prayer? The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn hinges on how we define the Quran—as Arabic words (nazm) or as meaning. Each side uses Quranic verses, qiyas, and principles like tawsi'ah (flexibility) to support their position. Let us analyse the reasoning behind each argument.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed premise and the point of dispute
Identify the consensus that a non-Arabic speaker may use Persian, and then the specific dispute: a person capable of Arabic.
2
Identify the proof of the opposing school (Sahibayn)
Locate the Quranic verses 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' and 'Inna anzalnahu quranan arabiyyan', and explain how they argue that Quran is Arabic speech.
The Sahibayn and three other Imams hold that recitation must be in Arabic.
3
Analyse Imam Abu Hanifa's first proof: tawsi'ah
Examine the argument that Allah permitted different Arabic dialects, showing the meaning is primary and words are flexible.
This leads to the conclusion that Persian is allowed with greater reason (qiyas awla).
4
Examine his second proof: facility in prayer
Note that recitation is dropped for the follower (muqtadi) to ease the prayer; similarly, the requirement of exact Arabic words can be eased.
This is a proof from the general spirit of flexibility in worship.
5
Evaluate the third proof: prayer as munajat
Understand the argument that prayer is an intimate conversation with Allah, where meaning suffices; this is a more spiritual reasoning.
This proof is attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa's deep spiritual state.

جواز کیلئے نظم کو رکن لازم نہیں قرار دیتے ۔ دلائل (1) لان یعنی اللفظ على التوسعة سے امام صاحب کی دلیل بیان فرمارہے ہیں امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ قرآن پاک کے الفاظ میں وسعت دو گنجائش و سہولت رکھی گئی ہے کیلئے لغت قریش میں تلاوت کرنا مشکل ہو گیا رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اللہ تعالی سے تخفیف کیلئے دعا فرمائی اللہ تعالی نے ہر قبیلہ کو اپنی لغت میں قراءت کرنے کی اجازت دے دی اور دوسرے قبیلہ کی لغت میں بھی تلاوت کرنے کی اجازت دے دی حتی کہ قریشی کیلئے اجازت تھی کہ وہ اپنی لغت چھوڑ کر دوسری لغت میں قراءت کر لے تو جب قریشی با وجود یکہ وہ اپنی لغت پر قادر ہے فصیح و بلیغ ہے اپنی لغت ترک کر کے دوسری لغت میں قراءت کر سکتا ہے تو غیر عربی کو جو اچھی طرح عربی پر قادر نہیں ہے عربی الفاظ ترک کر کے اپنی لغت میں بطریق اولی قراءت کرنے کی اجازت ہونی چاہئے اسی لیے امام صاحب نے غیر عربی کی قراءت کو جائز قرار دیا اور نظم عربی کو جواز صلوۃ کیلئے رکن لازم نہیں قرار دیا چونکہ یہ رخصت اسقاط ہے اس لیے عذر ہو یا نہ ہو دونوں صورتوں میں قراءت بالفارسیہ جائز ہوگی جس طرح سفر کی نماز رخصت اسقاط ہے عذر ہو یا نہ ہو۔ دلیل (۲) نماز کے اندر قرائت و تلاوۃ میں سہولت کو مد نظر رکھا گیا ہے یہی وجہ ہے مقتدی سے قراءت ساقط کر دی گئی ہے جیسا کہ ارشاد فرمایا گیا فاقرء واما تیسر من القران بسا اوقات ایک انسان کیلئے عربی میں قراءت کرنا معذور ہوتا ہے تو سہولت کیلئے نظم عربی کو لازم نہیں کیا گیا دلیل (۳) ، صاحب نور الانوار نے یہ دلیل بیان کی ہے کہ امام اعظم مستغرق في بحر التوحید تھے ان کا نظریہ تھا کہ حالت صلوۃ حالت مناجات ہے اس میں صرف ذات باری تعالی کی
His saying: 'Rather, consider the meaning because the basis of nazm is on flexibility and the meaning is the intended purpose, especially in the state of intimate conversation.' Purpose of clarifying the text: There is consensus among all imams that if a person cannot recite the Quran in Arabic, then reciting in non-Arabic in prayer is permissible. But if a person is capable of reciting in Arabic, can he recite in Persian or another language? There is a dispute. The madhhab of the three Imams and the Sahibayn is that he cannot recite in non-Arabic. Their proof is that recitation of the Quran in prayer is made a pillar and obligation. Allah says: 'So recite what is easy from the Quran.' And 'Quran' is the name of Arabic nazm, as established by texts: 'Indeed We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran' and 'An Arabic Quran.' So from these verses it is clear that the Arabic words are part of the Quran. [Unclear] It is necessary to recite Arabic nazm in prayer. Imam Abu Hanifah's madhhab is that if a person, without excuse and despite being capable of Arabic, recites in his own language and only conveys the meaning without reciting the words, his prayer will still be valid and the pillar of recitation will be fulfilled. So Imam Sahib does not consider nazm a necessary pillar for the validity of prayer. Proofs: (1) From 'because he said regarding flexibility' - he explains Imam Sahib's proof: He says that there is flexibility and ease in the words of the Quran, because the words themselves are not the goal; rather the main goal is the meaning. The words are a means to reach it. For this reason, the Quran was initially revealed in the dialect of Quraysh, which is the most eloquent language, but for other tribes reciting in Qurayshi dialect became difficult. The Prophet (peace be upon him) prayed to Allah for ease, and Allah permitted each tribe to recite in their own dialect, and also permitted reciting in another tribe's dialect. Even a Qurayshi was permitted to leave his own dialect and recite in another dialect. So if a Qurayshi, despite being capable of his own dialect and eloquent, can leave it and recite in another dialect, then a non-Arab who is not fully capable of Arabic should be allowed to leave Arabic words and recite in his own language with greater reason. Therefore Imam Sahib allowed recitation in non-Arabic and did not make Arabic nazm a necessary pillar for the validity of prayer. Since this is a rukhsat isqat (lenient concession), with or without excuse, recitation in Persian is permissible, just as the prayer of a traveler is a rukhsat isqat whether or not there is an excuse. (2) The second proof: Ease and facility are considered in recitation in prayer. For this reason, recitation is dropped from the follower, as Allah said: 'So recite what is easy from the Quran.' Sometimes a person is excused from reciting in Arabic, so for ease, Arabic nazm is not made necessary. (3) The third proof: The author of Nur al-Anwar stated that Imam A'zam was immersed in the ocean of tawhid; his view was that the state of prayer is a state of intimate conversation, in which only the essence of Allah... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents a significant fiqhi dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the permissibility of reciting the Quran in Persian (or another language) during prayer. The commentary first notes the unanimous agreement that a person unable to recite Arabic may recite in another language. The dispute arises when a person is capable of Arabic. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) along with the 'three Imams' (likely al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad) hold that reciting in non-Arabic is invalid; their proof is that the Quran is defined as Arabic speech, and the command 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Quran' requires the actual Arabic words. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, holds that the prayer is valid even if one recites only the meaning in Persian, provided the meaning is conveyed. His proofs are three: (1) The principle of tawsi'ah (flexibility) - Allah permitted recitation in different dialects, showing that the meaning is primary and words are flexible. By analogy, if a Qurayshi could switch dialects, a non-Arab can switch to Persian with greater reason. Moreover, this ruling is a rukhsat isqat (leniency that removes a requirement) and thus applies whether or not there is an excuse. (2) The principle of facility in prayer: recitation is dropped for the follower (muqtadi), so it can be replaced by meaning for the capable. (3) The nature of prayer as munajat (intimate conversation) with Allah, where the meaning suffices - a view attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa's spiritual station. It is important to note that while this passage presents Imam Abu Hanifa's position in detail, the later relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school follows the Sahibayn, requiring Arabic for those capable. The text does not explicitly state a tarjih (preference), but the commentator elaborates the proofs of Imam Abu Hanifa extensively.

Key Terms
قراءة بالفارسیۃ (qira'ah bil-Farsiyyah): Reciting the Quranic meaning in Persian language during prayer.
نظم عربی (nazm 'arabi): The ordered Arabic wording of the Quran.
توسعة (tawsi'ah): Flexibility or leniency in the Quranic text, allowing variation in expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is reciting in Persian invalid for a person capable of Arabic?
A) Because Persian is not a revealed language.
B) Because Quran is defined as Arabic nazm, and prayer requires reciting the actual Arabic words.
C) Because Imam Abu Hanifa prohibited it.
D) Because prayer must be silent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute on Persian recitation illustrates how usul principles (definition of Quran, tawsi'ah, qiyas) shape divergent rulings, with the later school preferring the cautious view of the Sahibayn.
